7h2.1044c1.5563,0,2.32,.6206,2.32,1.74,.0109,.539-.2936,1.0349-.7791,1.2692v.0242c.7243,.1716,1.2395,.8131,1.2509,1.5573,0,1.16-.6767,1.9652-2.5133,1.9652h-2.3828v-6.5559Zm2.0483,2.7588c.7414,0,1.1126-.2591,1.1126-.8893,0-.6767-.4456-.899-1.2799-.899h-.6109v1.7883h.7782Zm.2127,2.7898c.87,0,1.362-.2223,1.362-.9261s-.5007-.9667-1.4829-.9667h-.87v1.8908l.9908,.0019Zm4.9406-1.248l-2.32-4.3007h1.4548l1.5418,3.2267,1.6433-3.2248h1.4084l-2.4659,4.2726v2.2775h-1.2566l-.0058-2.2514Z" /></g></g></svg>        </a>

        <div class="row">
            <div class="col col-lg-8 pe-lg-0">
                                    <div class="article-header">
    <p class="category text-uppercase">ESET Research</p>
    <h1 class="page-headline">Stealth Falcon preying over Middle Eastern skies with Deadglyph</h1>    <p class="sub-title">ESET researchers have discovered Deadglyph, a sophisticated backdoor used by the infamous Stealth Falcon group for espionage in the Middle East</p>
    
    <div class="article-authors d-flex flex-wrap"><div class="article-author d-flex"><a href="/en/our-experts/eset-research/" title="ESET Research"><picture><source srcset="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/tn/-x45/wls/2022/03/twitter_profile_picture_400x400.png" media="(max-width: 768px)" /><img class="author-image me-3"  src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/tn/-x45/wls/2022/03/twitter_profile_picture_400x400.png" alt="ESET Research"  /></picture></a><div class="author-text"><p><a href="/en/our-experts/eset-research/" title="ESET Research"><b>ESET Research</b></a></p></div></div></div>
    <p class="article-info mb-5">
        <span>22 Sep 2023</span>
        <span class="d-none d-lg-inline">&nbsp;&bull;&nbsp;</span>
        <span class="d-inline d-lg-none">, </span>
        <span>21 min. read</span>
    </p>

    <div class="hero-image-container">
        <picture><source srcset="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/tn/-x266/wls/2023/2023-09/deadglyph/eset-research-stealth-falcon-deadglyph.jpeg" media="(max-width: 768px)" /><source srcset="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/tn/-x425/wls/2023/2023-09/deadglyph/eset-research-stealth-falcon-deadglyph.jpeg" media="(max-width: 1120px)" /><img class="hero-image"  src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/tn/-x700/wls/2023/2023-09/deadglyph/eset-research-stealth-falcon-deadglyph.jpeg" alt="Stealth Falcon preying over Middle Eastern skies with Deadglyph"  /></picture>    </div>
</div>
                    <div class="article-body">
    <p><span lang="EN-US">For years, the Middle East has maintained its reputation as a fertile ground for advanced persistent threats (APTs). In the midst of routine monitoring of suspicious activities on the systems of high-profile customers, some based in this region, ESET Research stumbled upon a very sophisticated and unknown backdoor that we have named Deadglyph. We derived the name from artifacts found in the backdoor (such as </span><span style="mso-bookmark: _Hlk143263082;"><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;">0x<strong>DEAD</strong>B001</span></span>, shown also in <!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Hlk143263082'></span><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Hlk143263082'><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'></span>REF _Ref111452440 \h <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span></span><![endif]--><span style="mso-bookmark: _Hlk143263082;"><span lang="EN-US">Table <span style="mso-no-proof: yes;">1</span><!-- [if gte mso 9]>
<![endif]--></span></span><!-- [if supportFields]><span style='mso-bookmark:
_Hlk143263082'></span><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span><![endif]--><span style="mso-bookmark: _Hlk143263082;"><span lang="EN-US">), coupled with the presence of a homo<strong>glyph</strong> attack. To the best of our knowledge, this is the first public analysis of this previously undocumented backdoor, used by a group that exhibits a notable degree of sophistication and expertise. Based on the targeting and additional evidence, we attribute Deadglyph with high confidence to the Stealth Falcon APT group.</span></span></p>
<p><span style="mso-bookmark: _Hlk143263082;"><span lang="EN-US">Deadglyph&rsquo;s architecture is unusual as it consists of </span></span><span lang="EN-US">cooperating components &ndash; one a native x64 binary, the other a .NET assembly. This combination is unusual because malware typically uses only one programming language for its components. This difference might indicate separate development of those two components while also taking advantage of unique features of the distinct programming languages they utilize. Different language can also be harnessed to hinder analysis, because mixed code is more difficult to navigate and debug.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">The traditional backdoor commands are not implemented in the backdoor binary; instead, they are dynamically received by it from the command and control (C&amp;C) server in the form of additional modules. This backdoor also features a number of capabilities to avoid being detected.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">In this blogpost, we take a closer look at Deadglyph and provide a technical analysis of this backdoor, its purpose, and some of the additional components we obtained. We are also presenting our findings about Deadglyph at the <a href="https://www.labscon.io/speakers/filip-jurcacko/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">LABScon 2023</a> conference.</span></p>
<blockquote>
<p><strong><span class="normaltextrun">Key points of the blogpost:</span></strong></p>
<ul>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpFirst"><em><span lang="EN-US">ESET Research discovered a sophisticated backdoor with unusual architecture that we have named Deadglyph.</span></em></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpMiddle"><em><span lang="EN-US">The main components are encrypted using a machine-specific key.</span></em></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpMiddle"><em><span lang="EN-US">Traditional backdoor commands are implemented via additional modules received from its C&amp;C server.</span></em></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpMiddle"><em><span lang="EN-US">We obtained three out of many modules &ndash; process creator, file reader, and info collector.</span></em></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpMiddle"><em><span lang="EN-US">We attribute Deadglyph to the Stealth Falcon group.</span></em></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpLast"><em><span lang="EN-US">Additionally, we found a related shellcode downloader; we postulate it could potentially be used for installation of Deadglyph.</span></em></li>
</ul>
</blockquote>
<p><span lang="EN-US">The victim of the analyzed infiltration is a governmental entity in the Middle East that was compromised for espionage purposes. A related sample found on VirusTotal was also uploaded to the file-scanning platform from this region, specifically from Qatar. The targeted region is depicted on the map in </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'></span>REF _Ref143614671 \h <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">Figure <span style="mso-no-proof: yes;">1</span><!-- [if gte mso 9]>
<![endif]--></span><!-- [if supportFields]><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-element:field-end'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">.</span></p>
<figure class="image"><img title="" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-09/deadglyph/deadglyph-figure-01.png" alt="Deadglyph Figure_01" />
<figcaption><em>Figure 1. Victimology of Deadglyph; the related sample was uploaded to VirusTotal from Qatar (in darker color)</em></figcaption>
</figure>
<p><span lang="EN-US">Stealth Falcon (also known as Project Raven or FruityArmor) is a threat group linked to the United Arab Emirates <a href="https://attack.mitre.org/groups/G0038/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">according to MITRE</a>. Active since 2012, Stealth Falcon is known to target political activists, journalists, and dissidents in the Middle East. It was first discovered and described by <a href="https://citizenlab.ca/about/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Citizen Lab</a>, which published an <a href="https://citizenlab.ca/2016/05/stealth-falcon/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">analysis</a> of a campaign of spyware attacks in 2016.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">In January 2019, Reuters published an <a href="https://www.reuters.com/investigates/special-report/usa-spying-raven/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">investigative report</a> on Project Raven, an initiative allegedly employing former NSA operatives and aiming at the same types of targets as Stealth Falcon. Based on these two reports referring to the same targets and attacks, Amnesty International <a href="https://twitter.com/botherder/status/1090590455048347649" target="_blank" rel="noopener">has concluded</a> (shown in </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
lang=EN-US><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'></span>REF _Ref144978712 \h <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">Figure <span style="mso-no-proof: yes;">2</span><!-- [if gte mso 9]>
<![endif]--></span><!-- [if supportFields]><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-element:field-end'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">) that Stealth Falcon and Project Raven actually are the same group.</span></p>
<figure class="image"><img title="" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-09/deadglyph/deadglyph-figure-2.png" alt="Deadglyph Figure 2" width="" height="" />
<figcaption><em>Figure 2. Claudio Guarnieri has connected Stealth Falcon with Project Raven</em></figcaption>
</figure>
<p><span lang="EN-US">In September 2019, we <a href="https://www.welivesecurity.com/2019/09/09/backdoor-stealth-falcon-group/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">published research</a> on a backdoor, attributed to Stealth Falcon, that used an unusual technique, <a href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/bits/background-intelligent-transfer-service-portal" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Background Intelligent Transfer Service</a>, for C&amp;C communication. We now reveal the result of our in-depth analysis of what presumably is the newest addition to Stealth Falcon&rsquo;s espionage toolset.</span></p>
<h2><span lang="EN-US">Deadglyph backdoor</span></h2>
<p><span lang="EN-US">Deadglyph&rsquo;s loading chain consists of multiple components, as illustrated in </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
lang=EN-US><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'></span>REF _Ref144978760 \h <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">Figure <span style="mso-no-proof: yes;">3</span><!-- [if gte mso 9]>
<![endif]--></span><!-- [if supportFields]><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-element:field-end'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">. The initial component is a registry shellcode loader, which loads shellcode from the registry. This extracted shellcode, in turn, loads the native x64 part of the backdoor &ndash; the Executor. The Executor subsequently loads the .NET part of the backdoor &ndash; the Orchestrator. Notably, the only component on system&rsquo;s disk as a file is the initial component, which is in the form of a Dynamic Link Library (DLL). The remaining components are encrypted and stored within a binary registry value.</span></p>
<figure class="image"><img title="" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-09/deadglyph/deadglyph-figure-02.png" alt="Deadglyph Figure_02" width="" height="" />
<figcaption><em>Figure 3. Deadglyph loading chain components</em></figcaption>
</figure>
<p><span lang="EN-US">While the precise method of the initial compromise vector is not yet determined, our suspicion is that an installer component is involved in deploying further components and establishing persistence within the system.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">In the rest of this section, we analyze each component.</span></p>
<h3><span lang="EN-US">Registry shellcode loader</span></h3>
<p><span lang="EN-US">Deadglyph&rsquo;s initial component is a tiny DLL with a single export, named </span><span class="Code"><span lang="EN-US">1</span></span><span lang="EN-US">. This component is persisted using <a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1546/003/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Windows Management Instrumentation (WMI) event subscription</a> and serves as a registry shellcode loader. It is executed via the command line </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">rundll32 C:\WINDOWS\System32\\pbrtl.dll,#1</span></span><span lang="EN-US">.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">The registry shellcode loader begins its operation by decrypting the path to the encrypted shellcode stored within the Windows registry, using RC4. We suspect the path is unique for each victim; in the case analyzed here, the registry path was:</span></p>
<p><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">Software\Classes\CLSID\{5abc7f42-1112-5099-b082-ce8d65ba0c47}\cAbRGHLg</span></span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">The root registry key is either </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;">HKLM</span><span lang="EN-US"> or </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;">HKCU</span><span lang="EN-US">, depending on whether the current process is running with elevated privileges or not. The same logic can be found in further components.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US" style="color: windowtext;">Following this, the loader derives a machine-specific RC4 key using the system UUID retrieved from the <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/sysinfoapi/nf-sysinfoapi-getsystemfirmwaretable" target="_blank" rel="noopener">raw SMBIOS firmware table</a>. Using this key, it loads, decrypts, and then executes the shellcode. It is important to highlight that this key derivation approach ensures that proper decryption won&rsquo;t occur if the loader is executed on a different computer.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">Interestingly, the loader can also be configured by a flag in its </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">.data</span></span><span lang="EN-US"> section to use a hardcoded key to decrypt the shellcode, instead of the machine-specific one.</span></p>
<p>We spotted a homoglyph attack mimicking Microsoft Corporation in the <span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;">VERSIONINFO</span> resource of this and other PE components. This method employs distinct Unicode characters that appear visually similar, but in this case not identical, to the original characters, specifically Greek Capital Letter San (U+03FA, Ϻ) and Cyrillic Small Letter O (U+043E, о) in <span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span style="background-color: #40e0d0;">Ϻ</span>icr<span style="background-color: #40e0d0;">о</span>s<span style="background-color: #40e0d0;">о</span>ft Corp<span style="background-color: #40e0d0;">о</span>rati<span style="background-color: #40e0d0;">о</span>n.</span></p>
<h3><span lang="EN-US">Registry shellcode</span></h3>
<p><span lang="EN-US">Comprised of two parts, the registry shellcode consists of a decryption routine and an encrypted body. First, the decryption routine rotates each byte of the encrypted body to the left by one (</span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">ROL 0x01</span></span><span lang="EN-US">). Subsequently, control is transferred to this decrypted body. The decrypted body consists of a PE loader and a PE file, the latter being the Executor, which represents the native part of the backdoor. This loader is responsible for parsing and loading the associated PE file.</span></p>
<h3><a name="_Toc143676261"></a><span style="mso-bookmark: _Ref119414808;"><span lang="EN-US">Executor</span></span></h3>
<p><span lang="EN-US">The Executor is the native x64 part of the Deadglyph backdoor, which does the following:</span></p>
<ul>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpFirst"><span lang="EN-US">loads its configuration,</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpMiddle"><span lang="EN-US">initializes the .NET runtime,</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpMiddle"><!--[endif]--><span lang="EN-US">loads the embedded .NET part of the backdoor (the Orchestrator), and</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpLast"><span lang="EN-US">acts as a library for the Orchestrator.</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span lang="EN-US">First, two default configurations embedded in the </span><span class="Code"><span lang="EN-US">.data</span></span><span lang="EN-US"> section are AES-decrypted. The configurations encompass various parameters, including encryption keys, safety and evasion settings, and the entry point of the subsequent component.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">During the initial execution, those two default configurations are stored within the Windows registry, from where they are loaded on subsequent runs, enabling the implementation of updates. The registry path for each configuration is generated with the following format:</span></p>
<p><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">{HKCU|HKLM}\Software\Classes\CLSID\{&lt;variable_GUID&gt;}\(Default)</span></span></p>
<p><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">&lt;variable_GUID&gt;</span></span><span lang="EN-US"> is a generated GUID, which is unique to each victim.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">Following this, the .NET runtime is initialized, then the Executor RC4-decrypts the .NET part of the backdoor known as the Orchestrator. The Orchestrator is located within the </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">.rsrc</span></span><span lang="EN-US"> section of the Executor. The configuration specifies the Orchestrator&rsquo;s execution method as an entry point. Moreover, a distinct structure is provided to facilitate accessibility of the Executor&rsquo;s functions by the Orchestrator.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">After launching the Orchestrator, the Executor acts as a support library for the Orchestrator. The Executor contains many interesting functions; we describe some of them in the following section, in context of their utilization by the Orchestrator and further loaded modules.</span></p>
<h3><span lang="EN-US">Orchestrator</span></h3>
<p><span lang="EN-US">Written in .NET, the Orchestrator is the main component of the Deadglyph backdoor. This component&rsquo;s primary role involves establishing communication with the C&amp;C server and executing commands, often facilitated through the intermediary role of the Executor. In contrast to the preceding components, the Orchestrator is obfuscated, employing .NET Reactor. Internally, the backdoor is referred to as </span><span class="Code"><span lang="EN-US">agent</span></span><span lang="EN-US">, which is a common name for the client part in various post-exploitation frameworks.</span></p>
<h4><span lang="EN-US">Initialization</span></h4>
<p><span lang="EN-US">The Orchestrator first loads its configuration and two embedded modules, each accompanied by its own set of configurations, from resources. These resources are <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deflate" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Deflate</a> compressed and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Advanced_Encryption_Standard" target="_blank" rel="noopener">AES</a> encrypted. They are referenced by an ID that is SHA-1 hashed into a resource name. An overview of these resources is provided in </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'></span>REF _Ref111452440 \h <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">Table <span style="mso-no-proof: yes;">1</span><!-- [if gte mso 9]>
<![endif]--></span><!-- [if supportFields]><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-element:field-end'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">.</span></p>

<p><span lang="EN-US">The description of Orchestrator configuration entries is shown in </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span lang=EN-US><span

</td>

</table>
</div></span></span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">After the resource components are loaded, multiple threads are created to carry out distinct tasks. One of these threads is responsible for conducting environment checks, a function implemented within the Executor. Another thread is devoted to establishing periodic communication with the C&amp;C server, enabling the retrieval of commands. Lastly, a set of three threads is employed for the purpose of executing received commands and subsequently transmitting any generated output back to the C&amp;C server.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">The environment-checking thread monitors running processes to identify unwanted ones. This thread operates with two distinct lists of process names. If a process on the first list is detected, C&amp;C communication and command execution is paused until the unwanted process no longer exists. If there is a match for any process on the second list, the backdoor immediately quits and uninstalls itself. </span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">Neither list was configured in the analyzed instance, so we don&rsquo;t know what processes might typically be checked for; we believe it is probably intended to evade analysis tools that could detect suspicious activity and lead to discovery of the backdoor.</span></p>
<h4><span lang="EN-US">Communication</span></h4>
<p><span lang="EN-US">The Orchestrator utilizes two embedded modules for C&amp;C communication &ndash; Timer and Network. Like the Orchestrator, these modules are obfuscated with .NET Reactor. The configuration for both modules is supplied by the Orchestrator. Within the Orchestrator, a preset configuration for the modules is included; optionally, the Orchestrator can also load an updated configuration version from the registry:</span></p>
<p><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">{HKCU|HKLM}\Software\Classes\CLSID\{&lt;variable_GUID&gt;}\&lt;mod_cfg_res_ID&gt;</span></span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">The backdoor contains an interesting safety measure related to communication. If the backdoor is unable to establish communication with the C&amp;C server for a duration surpassing a predefined threshold, configured within the Executor, a self-uninstallation mechanism is triggered. This time threshold is specified in hours and was set at one hour in the examined case.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">This approach serves a twofold purpose. On one hand, it prevents the generation of redundant network requests towards an inaccessible server. On the other hand, it reduces the chances of subsequent detection if the operators lose control over the backdoor.</span></p>
<h5><span lang="EN-US">Timer module</span></h5>
<p><span lang="EN-US">This small module executes the specified callback at a configurable interval. It is used by the Orchestrator in combination with the Network module to communicate with the C&amp;C server periodically. To prevent the creation of detectable patterns in network logs, the execution interval is subject to randomization, based on a percentage specified in the configuration. In the analyzed instance, the interval was set to five minutes, with a &plusmn;20% variation introduced for randomness.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">Another method to avoid detectable network patterns in periodic communication can be found in generation of requests sent to the C&amp;C server. This mechanism, implemented in the Executor, involves the inclusion of padding of varying length, comprised of random bytes, within the requests, resulting in requests of diverse sizes.</span></p>
<h5><span lang="EN-US">Network module</span></h5>
<p><span lang="EN-US">The Network module implements communication with the C&amp;C servers specified in its configuration. It can send data to a C&amp;C server using HTTP(S) POST requests. Notably, it offers several mechanisms to acquire proxy configuration details. This feature suggests a potential focus on environments where direct internet access is not available.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">An example of a decrypted (and beautified) configuration is shown in </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'></span>REF _Ref144978805 \h <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">Figure <span style="mso-no-proof: yes;">5</span><!-- [if gte mso 9]>
<![endif]--></span><!-- [if supportFields]><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-element:field-end'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">.</span></p>
<figure class="image"><img title="" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-09/deadglyph/deadglyph-figure-06.png" alt="Deadglyph Figure_06" width="" height="" />
<figcaption><em>Figure 5. Network module configuration</em></figcaption>
</figure>
<p><span lang="EN-US">Configuration entries contain details related to network communications &ndash; C&amp;C URLs, HTTP User-Agent, and optionally a proxy configuration.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">When communicating with the C&amp;C server, a custom binary protocol with encrypted content is used underneath HTTPS. </span></p>
<h4><span lang="EN-US">Commands</span></h4>
<p><span lang="EN-US">The Orchestrator receives commands from the C&amp;C server in the form of tasks, which are queued for execution. There are three kinds of tasks processed:</span></p>
<ul>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpFirst"><span lang="EN-US">Orchestrator tasks,</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpMiddle"><span lang="EN-US">Executor tasks, and</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpLast"><span lang="EN-US">Upload tasks.</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span lang="EN-US">The first two kinds are received from the C&amp;C server and the third is created internally to upload the output of commands and errors.</span></p>
<h5><span lang="EN-US">Orchestrator tasks</span></h5>
<p><span lang="EN-US">Orchestrator tasks offer the ability to manage the configuration of the Network and Timer modules, and also to cancel pending tasks. The overview of Orchestrator tasks is shown in </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
lang=EN-US><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'></span>REF _Ref111101783 \h <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">Table <span style="mso-no-proof: yes;">3</span><!-- [if gte mso 9]>
<![endif]--></span><!-- [if supportFields]><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-element:field-end'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">.</span></p>

<td style="width: 42.5pt; border-top: none; border-left: solid #BFBFBF 1.0pt; mso-border-left-themecolor: background1; mso-border-left-themeshade: 191; border-bottom: solid #BFBFBF 1.0pt; mso-border-bottom-themecolor: background1; mso-border-bottom-themeshade: 191; border-right: none; mso-border-top-alt: solid #BFBFBF .5pt; mso-border-top-themecolor: background1; mso-border-top-themeshade: 191; mso-border-left-alt: solid #BFBFBF .5pt; mso-border-bottom-alt: solid #BFBFBF .5pt; padding: .55pt 5.4pt .55pt 5.4pt; height: 22.7pt;" width="57"><code><code></code></code>

<code>

</table>
</div></span></p>
<h5><span lang="EN-US">Executor tasks</span></h5>
<p><span lang="EN-US">Executor tasks offer the ability to manage the backdoor and execute additional modules. It&rsquo;s notable that the traditional backdoor functionality is not inherently present within the binary itself. Instead, these functions are obtained from the C&amp;C server in the form of PE files or shellcode. The full extent of the backdoor&rsquo;s potential remains unknown without these additional modules, which effectively unlock its true capabilities. An overview of module tasks is shown in </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
lang=EN-US><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'></span>REF _Ref117677179 \h <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">Table <span style="mso-no-proof: yes;">4</span><!-- [if gte mso 9]>
<![endif]--></span><!-- [if supportFields]><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-element:field-end'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">, which includes details about the few identified modules. Similarly, </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
lang=EN-US><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'></span>REF _Ref117677188 \h <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">Table <span style="mso-no-proof: yes;">5</span><!-- [if gte mso 9]>
<![endif]--></span><!-- [if supportFields]><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-element:field-end'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US"> provides an overview of management tasks associated with the Executor.</span></p>

<p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 12.0pt;"><span lang="EN-US">The command that sets the Executor configuration can change the:</span></p>
<ul>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpFirst"><span lang="EN-US">unwanted process lists,</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpMiddle"><span lang="EN-US">time threshold of C&amp;C communication failure, and</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpLast"><!--[endif]--><span lang="EN-US">time limit for execution of additional modules.</span></li>
</ul>
<h6><span lang="EN-US">Modules</span></h6>
<p><span lang="EN-US">We managed to obtain three unique modules from the C&amp;C server, each corresponding to a different Executor task type, as shown in </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'></span>REF _Ref117677179 \h <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">Table <span style="mso-no-proof: yes;">4</span><!-- [if gte mso 9]>
 <w:data>08D0C9EA79F9BACE118C8200AA004BA90B02000000080000000E0000005F005200650066003100310037003600370037003100370039000000</w:data>
<![endif]--></span><!-- [if supportFields]><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-element:field-end'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">. Based on available information, we estimate there are nine to fourteen modules in total. As the modules are in fact backdoor commands, they have one basic operation to execute and then optionally return their output. The modules we obtained are DLLs with one unnamed export (ordinal </span><span class="Code"><span lang="EN-US">1</span></span><span lang="EN-US">), in which they resolve necessary API functions and call the main function.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">When executed, the modules are provided with an API resolution function, which can resolve Windows APIs and custom Executor APIs. The Windows APIs are referenced by a DWORD hash, calculated from the name of the API and its DLL. Small hash values (&lt;41) are treated specially, referencing the Executor API function. The Executor API comprises a total of 39 functions that are accessible to the modules. These functions pertain to a variety of operations, including:</span></p>
<ul>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpFirst"><!--[endif]--><span lang="EN-US">file operations,</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpMiddle"><span lang="EN-US">encryption and hashing,</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpMiddle"><span lang="EN-US">compression,</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpMiddle"><span lang="EN-US">PE loading,</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpMiddle"><span lang="EN-US">access Token Impersonation, and</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpLast"><span lang="EN-US">utility.</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span lang="EN-US">In the rest of this section, we describe the modules that we obtained.</span></p>
<h6><span lang="EN-US">Process creator</span></h6>
<p><span lang="EN-US">Module </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">0x69</span></span><span lang="EN-US"> executes the specified command line as a new process and provides the resulting output back to the Orchestrator. The process can be created under a different user, and its execution time can be limited. Notably, an unusual <a href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/jobapi2/">Job API</a> is used in this module&rsquo;s functionality.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">This module was served with the command line </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">cmd.exe /c tasklist /v</span></span><span lang="EN-US">.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">We assume it serves as an idle command issued automatically, while the operators wait for something interesting to happen on the compromised computer.</span></p>
<h6><span lang="EN-US">Info collector</span></h6>
<p><span lang="EN-US">Module </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">0x6C</span></span><span lang="EN-US"> collects extensive information about the computer via WMI queries and passes it back to the Orchestrator. Information about the following is collected:</span></p>
<ul>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpFirst"><!--[endif]--><span lang="EN-US">operating system,</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpMiddle"><span lang="EN-US">network adapters,</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpMiddle"><span lang="EN-US">installed software,</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpMiddle"><span lang="EN-US">drives,</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpMiddle"><span lang="EN-US">services,</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpMiddle"><!--[endif]--><span lang="EN-US">drivers,</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpMiddle"><span lang="EN-US">processes,</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpMiddle"><span lang="EN-US">users,</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpMiddle"><span lang="EN-US">environment variables, and </span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpLast"><span lang="EN-US">security software.</span></li>
</ul>
<h6><span lang="EN-US">File reader</span></h6>
<p><span lang="EN-US">Module </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">0x64</span></span><span lang="EN-US"> reads the specified file and passes the content back to the Orchestrator. Optionally, it can delete the file after reading.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">We saw this module used to retrieve the victim&rsquo;s Outlook data file </span></p>
<p><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">c:\Users\&lt;redacted&gt;\AppData\Local\Microsoft\Outlook\outlook.ost</span></span><span lang="EN-US">.</span></p>
<h3><a name="_Ref145415889"></a><span style="mso-bookmark: _Toc143676263;"><span lang="EN-US">Chain with shellcode downloader</span></span></h3>
<p><span lang="EN-US">In the process of investigating Deadglyph, we encountered a dubious CPL file signed with an expired certificate and no countersignature with a timestamp, which had been uploaded to VirusTotal from Qatar. Upon closer examination, it became evident that this CPL file functioned as a multistage shellcode downloader, sharing certain code resemblances with Deadglyph. The loading chain is illustrated in </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'></span>REF _Ref143693067 \h <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">Figure <span style="mso-no-proof: yes;">6</span><!-- [if gte mso 9]>
<![endif]--></span><!-- [if supportFields]><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-element:field-end'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">.</span></p>
<figure class="image"><img title="" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-09/deadglyph/deadglyph-figure-03.png" alt="Deadglyph Figure_03" width="" height="" />
<figcaption><em>Figure 6. Shellcode downloader loading chain</em></figcaption>
</figure>
<p><span lang="EN-US">In its initial form, which serves as the first stage, this file anticipates having a </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">.cpl</span></span><span lang="EN-US"> extension (Control Panel file) and is meant to be executed via a double-click action. Upon execution in this manner, the embedded shellcode undergoes XOR decryption, and the running processes are checked to identify a suitable host process for subsequent injection.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">If </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">avp.exe</span></span><span lang="EN-US"> (a Kaspersky endpoint security process) is running, </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">%windir%\system32\UserAccountBroker.exe</span></span><span lang="EN-US"> is used. Otherwise, the default browser is used. Then, it creates the host process in a suspended state, injects the shellcode by hijacking its main thread, and resumes the thread.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">The second stage, the shellcode, consists of two parts. The first part of the shellcode resolves API hashes, using the same unique hash calculation technique employed in Deadglyph, and decrypts strings with process names. It starts a self-delete thread tasked with overwriting and subsequently erasing the first-stage file. Following this, the shellcode proceeds to inspect the currently active processes, targeting a security solution.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">If any of the specified processes are detected, the shellcode creates a sleeper thread with the lowest priority (</span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">THREAD_PRIORITY_IDLE</span></span><span lang="EN-US">) and allows it to remain active for a duration of 60 seconds before terminating its operation. This interval is likely implemented as a precautionary measure to evade certain detection mechanisms employed by security solutions. Finally, the shellcode proceeds to invoke the execution of the second part of its code.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">The second part of the shellcode loads an embedded PE file with stage three and calls its export with ordinal number </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">1</span></span><span lang="EN-US">.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">The third stage, a DLL, serves as a .NET loader and contains the payload in its </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">.rsrc</span></span><span lang="EN-US"> section.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">To load the payload, the .NET runtime is initialized. During the .NET initialization, two intriguing techniques are performed, seemingly intended to evade Windows <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/amsi/antimalware-scan-interface-portal" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Antimalware Scan Interface (AMSI) scanning</a></span><span lang="EN-US">:</span></p>
<ul>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpFirst"><span lang="EN-US">The .NET loader temporarily hooks the<code> </code></span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">GetModuleHandleW</span></span><span lang="EN-US"> import in the loaded </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">clr.dll</span></span><span lang="EN-US">, while calling </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">ICorRuntimeHost::Start</span></span><span lang="EN-US">. The hook tampers with the return value when </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">GetModuleHandleW</span></span><span lang="EN-US"> is called with </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">NULL</span></span><span lang="EN-US">. It returns a pointer to a dummy PE with no sections.</span></li>
<li class="Bullets1CxSpLast"><!--[endif]-->It then subtly patches the <span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;">AmsiInitialize</span><span lang="EN-US"> import name string in the </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">.rdata</span></span><span lang="EN-US"> section of the loaded </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">clr.dll</span></span><span lang="EN-US"> to </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span style="background-color: #40e0d0;">a</span>msi<span style="background-color: #40e0d0;">i</span>nitialize</span><span lang="EN-US">.</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span lang="EN-US">The fourth stage is a .NET assembly, obfuscated with ConfuserEx, that serves as a shellcode downloader. First, it XOR-decrypts its configuration in XML format from its resources. A beautified version of the extracted configuration is presented in </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
lang=EN-US><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'></span>REF _Ref143695453 \h <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">Figure <span style="mso-no-proof: yes;">7</span><!-- [if gte mso 9]>
<![endif]--></span><!-- [if supportFields]><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-element:field-end'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">. The configuration entries contain details related to network communication and blocklisted processes.</span></p>
<figure class="image"><img title="" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-09/deadglyph/deadglyph-figure-05.png" alt="Deadglyph Figure_05" width="" height="" />
<figcaption><em>Figure 7. Shellcode downloader configuration</em></figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 12.0pt;"><span lang="EN-US">Before proceeding, it checks the running processes against a list of blocklisted processes from the configuration. If a match is detected, the execution halts. It is important to note that in the analyzed instance, this blocklist wasn&rsquo;t set up.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">Next, it sends an HTTP GET request to the C&amp;C server to retrieve some shellcode, using parameters specified in the configuration (URL, User-Agent, and optionally Proxy). Regrettably, during our investigation we were unable to acquire any shellcode from the C&amp;C server. Nonetheless, we hypothesize that the content being retrieved could potentially serve as the installer for Deadglyph.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">Following this, the retrieved shellcode is executed within a newly created thread. After waiting until the shellcode thread finishes execution, the shellcode downloader removes all files located in the directory </span><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">%WINDIR%\ServiceProfiles\LocalService\AppData\Local\Temp\TfsStore\Tfs_DAV</span></span><span lang="EN-US">.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">Finally, it makes an attempt to delete itself after a 20-second interval, employing the subsequent command, before concluding its operation and exiting:</span></p>
<p><span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;"><span lang="EN-US">cmd.exe choice /C Y /N /D Y /T 20 &amp; Del /f /q &lt;current_process_exe_path&gt;</span></span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">This self-deletion does not make sense in this chain. This is due to the fact that the shellcode downloader is executed within a browser or system process after being injected, rather than operating as an independent executable. Moreover, the initial file was already deleted by the second stage. This observation suggests that the shellcode downloader might not be an exclusive payload of this chain and may also be used separately in other operations.</span></p>
<h2><span lang="EN-US">Conclusion</span></h2>
<p><span lang="EN-US">We have discovered and analyzed a sophisticated backdoor used by the Stealth Falcon group that we have named Deadglyph. It has an unusual architecture, and its backdoor capabilities are provided by its C&amp;C in the form of additional modules. We managed to obtain three of these modules, uncovering a fraction of Deadglyph&rsquo;s full capabilities. </span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">Notably, Deadglyph boasts a range of counter-detection mechanisms, including continuous monitoring of system processes and the implementation of randomized network patterns. Furthermore, the backdoor is capable of uninstalling itself to minimize the likelihood of its detection in certain cases.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-US">Additionally, our investigation led us to the discovery of a compelling multistage shellcode downloader chain on VirusTotal. We suspect this downloader chain is likely leveraged in the installation process of Deadglyph.</span></p>
<blockquote>

For any inquiries about this service, visit the&nbsp;<a style="background-color: #f4f4f4;" href="https://www.eset.com/int/business/services/threat-intelligence/?utm_source=welivesecurity.com&amp;utm_medium=referral&amp;utm_campaign=wls-research&amp;utm_content=stealth-falcon-preying-middle-eastern-skies-deadglyph/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">ESET Threat Intelligence</a> page.</p>

<td style="width: 4.0cm; border: none; border-bottom: solid #BFBFBF 1.0pt; mso-border-bottom-themecolor: background1; mso-border-bottom-themeshade: 191; mso-border-top-alt: solid #A6A6A6 .5pt; mso-border-top-themecolor: background2; mso-border-top-themeshade: 166; mso-border-bottom-alt: solid #BFBFBF .5pt; padding: .55pt 5.4pt .55pt 5.4pt; height: 22.7pt;" width="151">
